The Evolution of Port City Performativity
This table presents a refined, performativity-focused typology of port city transformation across history. Each stage is defined by its dominant economic, technological, political, and cultural logics, but places special emphasis on key performative features—the actions, rituals, and routines that animate urban-port life and give meaning to the port city at every epoch.
________________________________________
Stages and Performative Logics
Stage	Timeframe	Key Performative Features	Economic Performance	Technological Performance	Political Performance	Cultural/Symbolic Performance
Mercantile	Medieval–19th c.	Merchant bargaining, auctions, public decrees, market haggling, blessing of ships, dockside storytelling	Trade, exchanges, emporia, banks, insurance, bills of exchange	Sailing ships, docks, warehouses, canals, shipyards, lighthouses, navigation tools	Merchant councils, city-state autonomy, port charters	Sailors’ tales, fairs, markets, religious celebrations, sea legends
Colonial-Extractive	16th–20th c.	Labor musters, colonial parades, tribute ceremonies, inspections, disciplinary rituals	Resource extraction, plantations, forced labor	Fortresses, surveillance, naval bases	Colonial law, direct rule, company sovereignty	Imperial pageantry, plantation rituals, authority displays, colonial propaganda
Industrial/Manufacturing	19th–mid-20th c.	Dockworker shifts, union rallies, industrial processions, labor strikes, machine inaugurations	Mass production, dock labor, urban industry	Steamships, railways, cranes, mechanized wharves	State planning, rise of unions, municipal expansion	Celebrations of technology, labor pride, civic parades
Petroleumscape	1920s–1970s	Tanker convoys, refinery openings, PR events, motoring rituals, oil-themed festivals	Oil trade, petro-finance, suburban growth	Refineries, pipelines, roads, petrochemical plants	State-corporate energy alliances, energy geopolitics	Oil as driver of modernity, spectacle, mass marketing
Deindustrial/Post-crisis	1970s–1990s	Site occupations, protest marches, commemorations, adaptive reuse projects, heritage walks	Economic decline, unemployment, rise of tourism	Decaying infrastructure, privatization, obsolescence	Urban protest, planning crises, new municipal strategies	Nostalgia, memory activism, ruin aesthetics
Containerization/Logistics	1960s–2000s	Container choreography, logistics meetings, security drills, efficiency contests	Global supply chains, logistics optimization	Container terminals, automation, multimodal hubs	Corporate-municipal partnerships, new regulations	Container as icon, efficiency narratives
Tourism/Pleasurescape	1980s–present	Waterfront festivals, cruise ceremonies, art fairs, public performances, night markets	Cruise economy, festivals, creative industries	Marinas, leisure infrastructure, digital booking	Place/destination marketing, event politics	Carnivals, urban branding, nostalgia
Blue/Green/Smart	21st c.–present	Participatory planning, climate drills, river clean-ups, co-created performances, blue parades	Circular/blue economy, innovation, ecosystem services	Renewables, blue-green infrastructure, sensors	Participatory governance, climate policy	Ocean as superactor, eco-heroism, resilience
Digital/Algorithmic	2010s–present	Real-time monitoring, algorithmic routines, cyber exercises, digital twin scenario testing	Platform capitalism, AI-driven logistics	Smart ports, IoT, blockchain, digital twins	Algorithmic governance, cybersecurity	Info-urbanism, smart city imaginaries
________________________________________
Note: Stages may overlap or follow different sequences depending on region and local history. This framework draws on Hoyle, Ducruet, Hein, Hein & van de Laar, Butler, Latour, and related scholarship.
Matrix of Port City Performativity: Practices and Qualities
This table outlines how port cities have evolved through different historical periods by identifying two key dimensions: concrete practices and performances (such as rituals, events, or actions) and their characteristic qualities (the tone, style, or atmosphere of these actions). By connecting what happens in port cities with how it happens, the matrix offers a deeper understanding of the shifting identities of port urbanity across time.
Stage	Example Practices and Performances	Descriptive Qualities
Mercantile (Medieval–19th c.)	Merchant negotiations, public auctions, ship blessings, harbor storytelling, trade fairs, sea pilgrimages	Negotiated, ceremonial, communal, open, adaptive, tradition-bound, spiritually influenced, cosmopolitan
Colonial-Extractive (16th–20th c.)	Forced labor gatherings, tribute ceremonies, colonial parades, plantation rituals, inspections	Authoritarian, hierarchical, militarized, symbolic, imposed, racialized, spectacular, ritualistic
Industrial/Manufacturing (19th–mid-20th c.)	Work shifts at docks, labor rallies, industrial parades, strikes, machine openings	Mechanized, organized, collective, productive, process-oriented, progressive, solidaristic
Petroleumscape (1920s–1970s)	Oil refinery openings, petro-parades, company PR events, car shows, consumption festivals	Corporate, suburbanized, energy-intensive, spectacular, growth-focused, promotional, consumer-oriented
Deindustrial/Post-crisis (1970s–1990s)	Occupations of abandoned areas, protest marches, heritage tours, reuse initiatives, memorial actions	Reflective, nostalgic, activist, bottom-up, adaptive, melancholic, commemorative, transitional
Containerization/Logistics (1960s–2000s)	Container terminal choreography, efficiency competitions, logistics planning, security exercises	Functional, standardized, automated, invisible, modular, impersonal, disciplined, networked
Tourism/Pleasurescape (1980s–present)	Cruise ship launches, urban festivals, public art installations, waterfront shows, night markets	Theatrical, curated, experience-driven, affective, inclusive, commodified, branded, recreational
Blue/Green/Smart (21st c.–present)	River cleanup events, participatory planning, resilience workshops, symbolic eco-performances	Sustainable, participatory, eco-conscious, future-oriented, inclusive, experimental, regenerative
Digital/Algorithmic (2010s–present)	Live data monitoring, digital simulations, cyber drills, blockchain applications	Predictive, automated, abstract, real-time, quantified, algorithm-driven, virtualized, systemic
________________________________________
This matrix is designed to help students and researchers analyze the changing performativity of port cities. It emphasizes both what port cities do (their key routines and enactments) and how those actions express broader social, political, technological, and environmental logics. Each historical stage reflects not only a different infrastructure or economy but a distinct way of organizing urban life around the port.
Performativity – A Contemporary Perspective
Performativity can be understood as a shared economic, technological, political, and cultural potential that shapes the cognitive framing of human actors. Through this framework, performers are able to plan intentional actions (such as performances), anticipating audience responses such as empathy, co-thinking, co-expression, co-responsibility, co-experience, co-presence, co-isolation, collaboration, or even co-protest. By submitting themselves to the evaluation of others, performers receive feedback as to whether their objectives, ideas, means of expression, stage, and scenography have been employed, enacted, experienced, and interpreted by the audience accurately, comprehensively, competently, and adequately.
Importantly, performativity extends beyond the realm of human activity; it also encompasses the possible performance schemata of non-human actors within various networks (ranging from nano- to hyper- entities), all of whom are entangled in intra-active co-responsibility for maintaining the balance between economy, technology, politics, and culture—in other words, the broader relationship among economy, society, and environment. The effects and meanings of the actions of both human and non-human actors thus possess a transcorporeal dimension, manifesting in networks of interactions where non-human entities (such as machines, infrastructures, organisms) are also active participants, co-creating the conditions for coexistence and shared responsibility.
Performativity is, therefore, a process of transcorporeality (intra-relational co-agency) involving not only human actors—those who co-act, co-experience, co-judge, co-share, co-express, and co-create, embodying norms, values, traditions, emotions, as well as adaptive, cognitive, interpretive, agentic, solidaristic, responsible, and reflexive abilities, along with principles of thrift, rationality, zero-emissions, non-toxicity, and non-invasiveness—but also in relations with non-human agents.
Performativity is a historically developed, overarching schema of intra-relationality that can be iteratively re-enacted in practice, reinterpreted through collective understanding, experience, and imagination, and creatively updated with new aspects and means of expression. Transcorporeality enables exploration, adaptation, experimentation, and transcendence of established schemas. The situational context, the more-than-human relational community, and the emotional state largely depend on whether, through the skill of the performer, transcorporeality is effectively utilized to communicate the performer's individual reinterpretation of the performance framework, allowing for the investigation of interconnections with other, also non-human, actors.
The public dimension of meaning is disclosed through action—be it economic, technological, political, or cultural performance—subjected to interpretation also by non-human actors (“more-than-human actors”), and based upon the requirement for an interpretive schema. This schema is grounded in shared reservoirs of practice and knowledge about the world, already codified by concepts and their linguistic designators; it also
1. Performativity Defined 
The concept of performativity refers to the idea that schemes of interpretation rely on performative features (profit, gain, efficiency, reliability dependability, power-status, agency characteristics entangled with expected arefacts, atributes, requisits, insignias, symbols used in action by human and more-than-human performers, that together with audience (port-city users), they all reproduce creatively or repeatedly present the intented-by-peformer and expected-by-public performative schemes by performance twist, where sensually experienced and performed agency, meets with emotional attchment, imagination, sensitivity and memories, to be finaly evaluated if a expected-by-performer-outcome was enacted, embodied, acknowledged, judged according to the recalled general frame and performativity. as a creative-interpretation repetition of a chosen scheme in repetuire of relations (J.L. Austin and later expanded by Judith Butler). 
2. Performativity of the City 
Cities are not static entities; they are continuously performed through the daily practices of their inhabitants. 
Urban spaces are shaped by repeated actions, cultural expressions, and social interactions. 
3. Performativity of the Port City 
In port cities, performativity is deeply tied to the interaction between maritime and urban environments. 
Port cities continuously perform their identity, image or mark through maritime culture, economic activities, technological efficiency and the dynamic relationship between sea and land. 
Cultural, technological, political and organizational performances are essential to understanding how these cities evolve and maintain their uniqueness (follow the actant).
The Performativity of the Blue Economy: Theoretical Synthesis
1. Conceptual Framework
Performativity in the blue economy refers to how economic, technological, political, and cultural practices not only represent but also constitute the realities of oceanic and port-city life. Drawing on performative studies, post-humanist translations, and Barad’s intra-action, the blue economy is understood as a dynamic, relational field where:
•	Performances (infrastructural, regulatory, everyday) enact new material and social realities.
•	Translations (post-humanism) reconfigure boundaries between human, non-human, and more-than-human actors, foregrounding the agency of water, infrastructure, and capital.
•	Intra-actions (Barad) emphasize that entities—ports, oceans, users, policies—do not pre-exist their relations but emerge through ongoing entanglements12.
This framework reveals the blue economy as a relational assemblage—not a static sector but a set of processes and enactments that materialize new forms of value, governance, and subjectivity across oceanic and urban interfaces.
2. The Blue Economy Across Five Axes
A. Global Ocean: Material and Imaginary
•	The ocean is both a resource frontier and a hyperobject (Morton), exceeding human scale and comprehension.
•	It is performed through global logistics, extractive practices, and imaginaries (e.g., “blue growth,” “sustainable ocean”)345.
•	Post-humanist readings see the ocean as an active participant, not a passive backdrop, shaping and being shaped by economic and technological flows12.
B. Transportation Supply Chain & Capital Flows (Economy)
•	90% of world trade moves by sea, making maritime logistics the backbone of the global economy6.
•	The blue economy is performative in generating new financial instruments (blue bonds, green shipping finance), supply chain innovations, and speculative futures7.
•	Capital flows intra-act with regulatory regimes, infrastructure upgrades, and environmental standards, producing new forms of value and risk87.
C. Aqua-Related Infrastructure (Technology)
•	“BlueTech” innovations (floating docks, desalination, renewable energy, marine biotechnology) are performative acts that transform both the urban and marine environment9.
•	Infrastructure is not neutral; it mediates intra-actions between water, technology, and users, reconfiguring urban metabolism and ecological relations910.
D. Multiscalar Portcity Governance (Politics)
•	Governance is enacted across scales: from EU policy to local port authorities, involving public-private partnerships and multi-level coordination1112.
•	The blue economy demands new governance architectures that are resilient, inclusive, sustainable, and circular (RISC-proof)1112.
•	Port cities are sites of negotiation, where global, national, and local interests intra-act, often producing both synergies and conflicts1314.
E. Port-City Interface Users (Culture)
•	Cultural performances (e.g., festivals, maker spaces, start-up incubators) enact new blue urban identities and foster cross-sectoral innovation1516.
•	Users—workers, citizens, entrepreneurs—translate blue economy narratives into everyday practices, shaping public perceptions and political legitimacy1516.
Case Study: Rotterdam as a Pattern Example of Blue City
Rotterdam’s Blue Economy Performativity
Dimension	Rotterdam Example	Performativity Manifested
Ocean-Economy	World’s largest port (by tonnage), global logistics hub, blue finance pilot projects	Materializes global supply chains, capital flows, and regulatory experiments15

Infrastructure	Blue City 010 incubator, floating farms, algae-based circular economy (Alga.farm)	Enacts technological and ecological innovation; repurposes urban space for blue growth1516

Governance	Multi-level partnerships (municipality, port authority, EU), RISC-proof policy frameworks	Negotiates between economic growth, sustainability, and social inclusion1112

Culture	Maker spaces, public engagement in water management, blue urbanism festivals	Fosters new identities and practices at the port-city-water interface1516

Rotterdam’s Performativity Patterns
•	Performances: Rotterdam’s transformation of abandoned infrastructure (e.g., Tropicana swimming pool into algae farm) is a performative act, re-signifying urban space and relations between city and water1516.
•	Translations: Start-up incubators and blue tech initiatives translate global sustainability goals into local practices, bridging post-humanist concerns with pragmatic innovation15.
•	Intra-actions: Governance, technology, and users are entangled in ongoing intra-actions—policy, investment, and everyday use co-produce the city’s blue identity and material reality111516.
3. Synthesis: Blue Economy as Performativity
•	The blue economy is not a fixed model but a set of ongoing performances, translations, and intra-actions that reconfigure the relations between ocean, economy, technology, governance, and culture.
•	Rotterdam exemplifies this performativity by continuously enacting new forms of blue urbanism, where the boundaries between land and water, human and non-human, economy and ecology are always in flux1516.
•	The performative lens reveals both the potential and the tensions of the blue economy: it can generate innovation and inclusion, but also risks of exclusion, conflict, and ecological degradation if not critically managed1314.
4. Critical Limitations
•	Power Asymmetries: Not all actors benefit equally; global capital and large corporations often dominate blue economy narratives and resources1314.
•	Ecological Risks: The performativity of growth can mask ongoing environmental degradation and social exclusion if not checked by robust governance and public engagement1113.
•	Translation Gaps: Post-humanist and intra-action frameworks highlight the need to recognize non-human agency, but practical implementation remains challenging in policy and planning12.

All claims are supported by sources as indicated in the inline citations.

"Resource Frontier": Definition, Origins, and Key Sources
Definition
A resource frontier refers to a geographical area—often at the periphery of established economic or political centers—that is newly opened or targeted for resource extraction. These frontiers are typically remote, underdeveloped, and characterized by the initial stages of economic transformation, where natural environments are appropriated and converted into sites for mineral, energy, agricultural, or marine resource exploitation12.
Who Coined the Term?
•	The term "resource frontier" in its contemporary sense is most prominently associated with Anna Tsing. In her influential 2003 work, Tsing explicitly coins "resource frontiers" to describe zones of accelerated resource "discovery" and extraction, especially in the context of late-20th-century global expansion34.
•	Earlier, the concept of "frontier" as a zone of expansion and extraction was discussed in colonial and economic geography, but Tsing’s work marks a decisive shift to the postcolonial, posthuman, and ecological context of resource extraction.
Key Sources and Theoretical Anchors
Author / Source	Contribution / Context	Citation
Anna Tsing (2003)	Coins "resource frontiers" as sites of accelerated extraction, mapping the socio-ecological impacts	34

John Friedmann	Describes resource frontiers as "thinly populated margins" settled for extraction, not just habitation	5

Barbier (2012); Lambin et al. (2013); Meyfroidt et al. (2018)	Analyze resource frontiers as transformative moments in resource discovery and institutional change	6

Society for the Anthropology of Work (SAW)	Traces the genealogy of "frontier" from colonial boundary-making to zones of resource exploitation	7

Oxford Reference	Defines resource frontier as a region dominated by extraction, distant from economic/population cores	8

Tutor2U, SaveMyExams	Educational summaries—resource frontiers as peripheral, newly-exploited areas (e.g., Arctic, deep sea)	12

Notable Theoretical Expansions
•	Ocean as Frontier: The application of the frontier concept to the ocean, especially in blue economy contexts, is explored by Steinberg (2018) and others, who note how the ocean is increasingly seen as a new resource frontier, both materially (e.g., seabed mining) and imaginatively (as a zone of opportunity and regulation)59.
•	Posthuman and Political-Ecological Readings: Tsing and subsequent scholars emphasize that resource frontiers are not only economic but also sites of intense social, ecological, and political transformation—often involving the displacement of local communities and reconfiguration of governance regimes734.
Summary Table: Key Sources
Source / Author	Year	Key Contribution
Anna Tsing	2003	Coins "resource frontier" for late-20th-century extraction zones34

John Friedmann	1990s	Resource frontiers as margins of national territory5

Barbier, Lambin, Meyfroidt et al.	2012+	Resource frontiers as transformative institutional moments6

Steinberg	2018	Ocean as frontier: opening/closure dynamics5

Oxford Reference, Tutor2U	2017+	Definitions and educational context812

In summary:
The term "resource frontier" was notably coined and theorized by Anna Tsing in 2003, though its conceptual roots reach back to earlier economic and colonial geography. It has since become a foundational concept in political ecology, anthropology, and blue economy studies, particularly for analyzing the shifting boundaries and socio-ecological impacts of global resource extraction345.

Performances in the Blue Economy
Understanding "Performances" in the Blue Economy
In the context of the blue economy, performances refer to the enacted practices, innovations, and socio-technical arrangements that actively shape and materialize the interactions between oceanic resources, economic sectors, governance, technology, and culture. These performances are not merely outcomes but are constitutive acts that create new realities—economically, ecologically, and socially—across the port-city-ocean interface.
Key Dimensions of Blue Economy Performances
1. Economic and Sectoral Performances
•	Growth and Recovery: The blue economy has demonstrated robust performance, particularly in the EU, with direct employment of 4.82 million people and nearly €890 billion in turnover in 2022. Sectors such as maritime transport, renewable energy, and coastal tourism have shown strong increases in both gross value added (GVA) and employment123.
•	Emerging Sectors: Offshore wind energy, blue biotechnology, and desalination are key areas of innovation, with offshore wind showing a 42% increase in GVA compared to 202112.
•	Circular and Sustainable Practices: The blue economy is increasingly characterized by low-polluting, resource-efficient, and circular economic models that emphasize sustainable consumption and production patterns45.
2. Infrastructural and Technological Performances
•	BlueTech Innovations: The development and deployment of floating docks, renewable energy platforms, and marine biotechnology exemplify performative acts that transform both urban and marine environments34.
•	Urban-Water Integration: Blue urbanism initiatives prioritize water transit, floating platforms, and resilient urban design, integrating ecological principles into city planning and infrastructure67.
3. Governance and Political Performances
•	Multi-level Governance: Cities and regions play a pivotal role in fostering resilient, inclusive, sustainable, and circular (RISC-proof) blue economies. This involves establishing governance conditions that support policy coherence and effective implementation8.
•	Port-City Interface Management: The port-city interface is managed as a dynamic threshold, balancing economic functions with urban and ecological needs. This space acts as a filter, meeting point, and site for innovative governance experiments910.
4. Cultural and Social Performances
•	Public Engagement and Identity: Cultural performances—such as festivals, maker spaces, and public water management initiatives—help enact new blue urban identities and foster innovation at the port-city-water interface76.
•	Knowledge Sharing and Collaboration: Case studies highlight the importance of sharing experiences, tools, and results among stakeholders to promote transitions toward sustainable blue economies45.
Table: Performances Across Blue Economy Axes
Axis	Performance Example	Impact Manifested
Economic	Offshore wind energy growth (+42% GVA in 2022)12
Drives innovation and investment
Infrastructure/Technology	Floating platforms, BlueTech incubators36
Urban-marine integration, resilience
Governance	RISC-proof frameworks, multi-level partnerships89
Inclusive, adaptive governance
Culture	Blue urbanism festivals, public engagement76
New identities, social innovation
Case Study Highlight: Rotterdam
Rotterdam exemplifies performative blue economy practices through:
•	Transformation of Infrastructure: Repurposing abandoned urban spaces (e.g., Tropicana swimming pool into an algae farm) as performative acts that re-signify city-water relations.
•	Innovation Ecosystems: Blue City 010 incubator and circular economy projects enact technological and ecological innovation.
•	Governance Experiments: Multi-level governance and RISC-proof policy frameworks negotiate between economic growth, sustainability, and inclusion.
•	Cultural Initiatives: Public engagement in water management and blue urbanism events foster a sense of shared identity and stewardship.
Critical Reflections
•	Performances are not neutral: They can reinforce power asymmetries and ecological risks if not critically managed.
•	Translation and Intra-action: The performativity of the blue economy is continually shaped by the translations and intra-actions among human and non-human actors, technologies, and policies, demanding ongoing reflexivity and adaptation.
In summary:
Performances in the blue economy are dynamic, multi-scalar enactments that materialize new forms of value, governance, technology, and culture at the intersection of ocean, city, and port. These performances are central to both the opportunities and tensions inherent in blue economy transitions123845796.

Appendix: Port City Performativity Framework (Theoretical Block)
Theoretical Foundations of Performativity
•	Speech Act Theory: J.L. Austin introduced performative utterances—statements that enact an action when spoken (Austin, 1962). Saying "I name this ship..." does the naming. Language here is not merely descriptive but world-making.
•	Social Performance: Erving Goffman (1959) and Richard Schechner emphasized everyday life as performance. Social actions draw on “restored behaviors” (patterns repeated and reinterpreted) rather than spontaneous acts (Schechner, 1988). Behavior display is a “fundamental category of human life” – echoing Shakespeare’s “all the world’s a stage.”
•	Identity Performativity: Judith Butler argued gender and other identities are performed through repeated acts. “Gender is an identity constituted in time through a stylized repetition of acts,” not a pre-given essence (Butler, 1990). In other words, consistent performative behaviors create the effect of an identity (Butler, 1993).
•	Material Performativity: Karen Barad extends performativity beyond discourse to the material world. Reality is enacted through intra-action – the mutual constitution of entities through their relations. Objects and bodies do not pre-exist interaction; rather, phenomena emerge “within intra-acting agencies” (Barad, 2007). Matter itself is “not a thing but a doing, a congealing of agency,” an ongoing active process. This agential realism challenges the subject–object split, showing that meaning and matter are co-constituted (Barad, 2007).
•	Actor–Network Theory (ANT): Bruno Latour, Michel Callon and others reconceptualize society as networks of human and non-human actors. Agency is distributed across assemblages of people, organisms, technologies, etc. (Latour, 2005; Callon, 1986). No single actor has primacy; effects are produced through the networked performance of heterogeneous elements. This underpins a relational view of cities and ports as emergent from actor-networks rather than fixed structures.
Port City Performativity – Key Concepts
•	Port City Performativity (Definition): A dynamic, constitutive process in which urban–port–ocean relations are continuously enacted, negotiated, and transformed by myriad actors (human and more-than-human) in economic, technological, political, and cultural domains. The port city is not a static backdrop but is “always in the making,” produced through daily practices and interventions rather than just represented.
•	Constitutive Practices: Focus on actions, infrastructures, and policies that make the port city what it is (vs. merely reflecting it). Performativity implies reality is actively shaped by doing. For example, building a floating dairy farm enacts a new urban-ocean relation rather than just symbolizing one. In this view, practice precedes structure – the city’s identity is an outcome of performative acts (Taylor, 1999).
•	Relational Assemblage: The port city emerges from entanglements of people, water, technology, goods, nature. It is a network assemblage (Deleuze & Guattari, 1987) where boundaries are blurred. Actors intra-act such that “individuals only exist within phenomena… in ongoing intra-active reconfiguring” (Barad, 2007). Intra-action means port, city, and ocean co-define one another through their interactions, rather than being separate entities that interact externally.
•	More-than-Human Agency: Non-human elements (ships, water, algae, animals, algorithms) are not passive background; they actively co-produce outcomes. A port city’s transformation involves cows on a floating farm, or algae in a bioreactor, as agents in the urban network (Bennett, 2010). This recognizes posthuman performativity (Barad, 2003) – agency emerges in collectives of humans and non-humans. E.g., “cows, robots, and rainwater systems co-perform food production on water,” demonstrating an assemblage of lively actors.
•	Material–Discursive Nexus: Ideas, narratives, and discourses (e.g. a city’s “sustainability” branding) are intertwined with material interventions (e.g. installing solar panels on a port warehouse). Performativity collapses the divide – talk and tech together enact reality. A policy or cultural narrative can be performative if it materializes new practices. “Matter is not mere stuff… matter is substance in its iterative intra-active becoming” – thus, a vision like “Green Port” becomes real through material projects (renewable energy, circular economy hubs) coupled with discourse (blue economy rhetoric).
•	Ongoing Becoming: The port city is perpetually in flux. Performativity emphasizes process over product: each adaptation (new policies, new infrastructure, new uses of space) reconfigures the port-city assemblage. There is no final state – “port city is always in the making, never fixed”. Temporal and spatial boundaries remain fluid (Massey, 2005), much like the tidal rhythms that shape port operations. Change is not linear but iterative, with repetition and difference producing new configurations over time.
Analytical Framework: Performativity Schema
•	Holistic Domains: We apply the performativity lens across four interrelated domains – Economic, Technological, Political, and Cultural. Each domain represents a set of practices and actors through which the port city is continuously performed and transformed. This schema ensures a comprehensive analysis: from material economies to governance structures to cultural imaginaries, all treated as performative arenas that intra-act.
•	Domain Interdependence: These domains are analytically distinct but empirically intertwined. Economic practices rely on technologies; technological innovations provoke cultural shifts; political frameworks enable or constrain economic and ecological actions, etc. In the port-city context, changes often emerge at the interfaces of domains (e.g. a technological innovation like offshore energy requires political regulation and sparks cultural acceptance). An actor–network viewpoint highlights that networks cut across domains, weaving economic, technical, political, and cultural threads into the fabric of port city transformation (Latour, 2005).
•	Intra-Action Across Scales: Performativity in each domain operates at multiple scales – from local actors (entrepreneurs, community groups) to global structures (markets, international treaties). Through intra-action, local and global are mutually constitutive. For instance, a local algae farm project ties into global circular economy narratives (economic domain) and EU sustainability agendas (political domain), reshaping cultural attitudes toward waste (cultural domain) via technological means (bioreactors). The framework thus captures scalar performativity: how actions at the human scale (or micro scale) are entangled with macro-scale structures in producing change (Marston et al., 2005).
•	Performative Outcomes: By mapping these domains, we can identify performative constellations – configurations where specific practices in each domain converge to produce a new reality (e.g., Rotterdam’s “Blue City” identity). Rather than attributing change to a single cause, this schema shows it emerges from the synchronization of performative acts across domains. The end goal is to reveal the process logic of transformation: how economic innovations, technological apparatus, governance decisions, and cultural narratives collectively enact the Blue Economy paradigm in the port city.
Paradigm Shift: From Containerization to Blue Economy
•	“Containerization” Paradigm (Past): The late-20th-century port city was defined by industrial-modernist logic – specialization, efficiency, and spatial segregation. Ports expanded seaward and became isolated enclaves of logistics. Rotterdam exemplified this “extreme port-city separation”, where port functions moved out and walled themselves off from the urban core (Hein & Van de Laar, 2020). Key traits of this paradigm:
o	Spatial Logic: Separation – distinct zones for port vs. city, often buffered by fences or distance. Water was treated as a barrier between city life and port operations.
o	Agency Focus: Human–Machine Efficiency – the emphasis on cranes, containers, and ships optimized for throughput. Non-human nature was largely background or obstacle (water to be tamed, dredged, or bridged).
o	Economic Model: Linear & Extractive – based on global trade flows, resource throughput and waste externalization (throwaway economy). Success measured in volume and speed, not environmental integration.
o	Governance: Technocratic & Sectoral – port authorities and city authorities operated separately; policy aimed at growth of port as an economic engine, with little consideration of urban livability or ecology (Hall, 1987). The port-city interface became a rigid barrier – socially and physically.
•	“Blue Economy” Paradigm (Emergent): In the 21st century, a new model is unfolding that re-integrates port and city through sustainability and innovation. The Blue Economy is defined as “the sustainable use of ocean resources for economic growth, improved livelihoods and jobs, while preserving ocean health” (World Bank, 2017). In port cities, this translates to a holistic approach bridging maritime industry with urban environment (Steinberg & Peters, 2020). Key traits:
o	Spatial Logic: Integration & Permeability – blurred boundaries between port and city. Waterfronts are redeveloped as mixed-use “innovation ecosystems” rather than purely cargo zones. Water is seen as a connector (a blue corridor), enabling urban expansion into the water (floating structures, tidal parks) instead of the old land-reclaim-and-separate model. The port-city interface becomes a porous threshold for exchange and cohabitation, not a fence.
o	Agency Assemblage: More-than-Human Assemblages – recognition that ecosystems (wetlands, algae, marine life) and technologies (sensors, AI, platforms) together drive innovation. The port city is managed as an eco-technical system. E.g., tidal energy and algae farms are integrated into urban infrastructure, giving non-human nature a direct performing role in the economy (e.g., algae sequestering CO₂ while becoming biofuel feedstock). Human decision-makers now work alongside “lively” matter and intelligent machines (Latour, 2017).
o	Economic Model: Circular & Regenerative – follows principles of circular economy (Stahel, 2016). Waste streams are cycled into inputs; industries symbiotically share resources. Value creation is multi-capital (economic, social, natural). In Rotterdam’s BlueCity, for example, coffee waste becomes substrate for mushroom farming, whose waste feeds algae farming, in a loop that generates profit and zero waste (circular performative value creation). Growth is redefined to include resilience and environmental gain, not just GDP.
o	Governance: Collaborative & Multi-Scalar – city, port authority, national and EU bodies co-create policies (public-private partnerships, participatory planning). “Multi-level governance experiments” enact sustainability goals through pilot projects. Regulation becomes iterative and adaptive, treating policies as performative instruments to guide behavior (e.g., Rotterdam’s climate adaptation strategy uses experiments like Water Plaza to demonstrate new practices). The Blue Economy blueprint relies on constant negotiation and learning between scales (from UN ocean targets down to neighborhood initiatives).
This shift is essentially performative: it required performing new relations into being. Rotterdam’s case shows an active reversal of separation – “extreme separation… being reversed through innovative integration strategies”. Instead of merely planning integration on paper, actors in Rotterdam performed integration: they built floating farms and tidal parks, launched collaborations (e.g. the Port Authority with startups and universities), and rebranded the city as a maritime innovator. The result is a paradigm change not through abstract policy alone, but through cumulative enactments on the ground.
Wet Ontologies and Blue Urbanism: Rethinking City–Ocean Relations
•	Thinking With the Sea: Traditional urban theory is terra-centric – it takes land as the default ontology (solid, static, partitioned). Wet ontology (Steinberg & Peters, 2015) flips this perspective, asking how the ocean’s traits – fluidity, depth, mobility – can reshape how we understand space. The sea is a “three-dimensional, wet, volatile form – with ongoing processes of repetition and differentiation, dissolution and recomposition, stasis and dynamism”. Embracing this ontology means seeing urban space as volumetric and dynamic (not flat and static) and city-water relations as constantly reworked. In practice: a wet ontological view encourages designs like amphibious architecture, tidal parks, and floating urban districts that accept and work with water’s movement.
•	Intra-action of Wet and Dry: Karen Barad’s concept of intra-action pairs with wet ontology – land and water are not separate categories with a fixed boundary (coastline) between, but intra-actively define each other. The port city is an interface phenomenon: a hybrid of wet and dry spaces. Blue Urbanism (Beatley, 2014) builds on this by integrating marine ecosystems into urban planning. Rather than ending at the shore, the city’s planning domain extends outward to include “blue belts” of protected and productive ocean space (Beatley, 2014). For example, Rotterdam’s urban planners now consider the Maasvlakte seawalls and North Sea wind farms as part of the metro’s infrastructure ecosystem.
•	Beyond Binaries: Wet ontology also destabilizes binaries like land/sea, human/nature, city/port. It argues for a continuum or a relational space. The port-city is a prototypical wet ontology space: partly land (infrastructure, warehouses), partly water (ships, channels), with constant exchange between. Viewing it this way fosters innovation – e.g., turning a former harbor basin into a “water plaza” that both stores water (an ecological function) and provides public space (an urban function). This blurring is a performative act of redefinition: water becomes an active urban element (a street when dry, a reservoir when wet) rather than a threat or void.
•	Cultural Shift – Blue Imaginaries: Steinberg & Peters (2015) note that adopting a wet ontology opens “new frames for thinking” in social sciences. Culturally, this means developing a maritime mindset in urban populations – citizens see themselves as residents of an “ocean world” as much as of a city. Rotterdam’s educational programs and events (e.g., maritime festivals, BlueCity workshops) help cultivate this by physically engaging people with water and marine life. The result is a budding blue urban culture: public support for marine conservation, pride in the port’s ecological innovation, and new narratives (e.g. Rotterdam as “the city that embraces the sea” rather than fights it). This cultural performativity is crucial: policy and tech changes require matching shifts in values and identities. Blue Urbanism thus is performative at heart – it asks urban communities to perform care for the ocean in their daily life and civic actions (from beach clean-ups to voting for blue policies).
Economic Performativity Domain
•	Reconfiguring Value: Economic performativity in the port city context means that market values and economic outputs are not pre-given but enacted through practice. New business models perform a sustainable economy into being. Example: Circular economy initiatives in Rotterdam turn waste into value, thereby performing a shift in what counts as a resource. At BlueCity (the hub in a former tropical swimming complex), entrepreneurs take one another’s by-products as inputs. Coffee waste → mushroom farm substrate → spent mushroom compost → algae feedstock → spirulina products. By materially doing this, they enact a economy where “waste is not waste” but a cycle. Each transaction and experiment constitutes the circular market (Callon, 1998). The result: the meaning of “economic value” expands to include ecological and social value, performed by these daily practices.
•	Non-Human Inputs in Markets: Traditional economics treats nature as external, but here algae, fungi, and bacteria become market participants. Intra-action: Algae in AlgaFarm are “active, generative partners” in production, not just raw materials. This blurs the line between economic actor and environmental element. By incorporating living organisms intentionally, the economy is performed as a bioeconomy. For instance, Rotterdam’s AlgaFarm integrates algae’s biological agency (growth responding to sun/nutrients) into its profit model – a performative alignment of ecology and economy (Pauli, 2010).
•	Constitutive Metrics: New performance metrics are introduced, which are performative themselves. Instead of just throughput tonnage or GDP, success is measured in circular value (e.g. “€1.5M+ in circular value chains created by algae-based products”). By reporting such metrics, actors reinforce the reality of a blue economy. These indicators make real the concept of “blue value” (a term used in port-city studies). The framing of profit includes sustainability outcomes – effectively performing a narrative that economy and ecology are not at odds but synergistic (Geissdoerfer et al., 2017).
•	Key References: Callon (1998) on economic markets as networks of performative processes; Pauli (2010) on the Blue Economy concept; Kirchherr et al. (2017) on circular economy principles; Lo et al. (2020) mapping BlueCity’s value flows; Bennett (2010) on vibrant materiality in economic systems.
Technological Performativity Domain
•	Hybrid Assemblages: Technological performativity views technology not as a neutral tool but as an active mediator that co-shapes social and material relations (Latour, 1992). In Rotterdam’s port city transformation, technologies (from sensors to floating platforms) are part of assemblages with humans and other life. A prime example is the Floating Farm: an offshore dairy farm where cows, farmers, robots, AI systems, solar panels, and the water itself form a production network. This is a “human–machine–nature” assemblage performing new food production methods. The technology (robotic milking, automated manure processing) doesn’t simply improve efficiency; it redefines farming by enabling it to happen on water, near the city. The farm performs climate adaptation (it floats with sea level) and local food production simultaneously, showing technology as a bridge between urban demands and aquatic environment.
•	Mediation and Agency: Technologies in the port city act as mediators that alter the actors’ possibilities (Verbeek, 2005). For instance, the introduction of digital sensors on port quays allows the water to “speak” by providing data (tides, pollution levels) – effectively giving the non-human environment a voice in decision-making. Technology thus extends agency to new actants (water, weather, traffic flows). In performative terms, each new tech deployment is an intervention that restructures the network of relations: e.g., installing a hydrogen pipeline from a new 200MW electrolyzer plant means the port’s layout and operations adjust to treat renewable energy as a core asset (Shell’s Holland Hydrogen I project). This performs decarbonization: the physical pipeline and electrolyzer materialize the abstract goal of energy transition.
•	Co-Design with Nature: Technological performativity in a blue economy often means designing with ecological principles. Nature-based solutions (like oyster reefs for wave attenuation, or algae bioreactors for wastewater treatment) are techniques that incorporate living systems. These blur the line between tech and organism – a performative merging of domains. They also require a shift in engineering culture: engineers become “stage managers” of multi-species performances (e.g., creating conditions for oysters to thrive and protect the shore). The success of such projects (e.g., Building with Nature initiatives in Dutch ports) is measured by how well human design and natural processes co-act.
•	Adaptation and Feedback: The performativity framework emphasizes that technologies in the port city are part of feedback loops – they adapt and evolve through use, and users adapt with them (Pickering, 1995). For example, Rotterdam’s new storm barrier systems (Maeslantkering) not only physically protect the city but also change how policymakers plan for risk (political domain) and how citizens perceive climate threat (cultural domain). The technology’s presence performs a sense of security that enables further waterfront development – a performative effect beyond its mechanical function. In turn, as climate risks escalate, the technology might be used differently (closing more often, etc.), showing ongoing intra-action between technical system and environmental forces.
•	Key References: Latour (1992, 2005) on technologies as actors; Callon (1986) on socio-technical networks; Haraway (2016) on cyborg urban ecologies; Verbeek (2005) on technological mediation; Bijker & Law (1992) on shaping of technology in society.
Political Performativity Domain
•	Governance as Performance: Politics in the port city is not only in policies and plans but in their enactment. The performative lens sees governance arrangements – laws, institutions, partnerships – as continually performed into being through negotiations, agreements, and public demonstrations of legitimacy. In Rotterdam’s blue transition, multi-level governance is key. The city and port joined European and national programs (e.g. EU’s Horizon projects) to secure funding and mandate for innovation. These projects (such as MAGPIE for green ports) serve as performative stages where policy ideas (like zero-emission shipping) are tested and demonstrated. For instance, the MAGPIE project’s hydrogen bunkering pilot – transferring 800 m³ of liquid hydrogen-derived fuel between ship and shore – was a public performance of policy. By successfully executing the demo, partners enacted the viability of hydrogen fueling, effectively making policy real and building momentum for regulatory change.
•	Multi-Scalar Networks: Political performativity recognizes the scalar choreography in port governance. International bodies (IMO, EU) set broad agendas (e.g., carbon-neutral shipping by 2050). These only materialize via local performances: a city hosting a conference, a port authority initiating a pilot, a province creating a new incentive. Each scale’s actors must align, which involves continuous performative acts like translation of goals (Callon, 1986) – e.g., translating EU directives into a city roadmap with visible projects. Rotterdam’s government and port authority effectively act as stage directors bringing together actors (businesses, NGOs, academics) in task forces and coalitions. Their convening power and strategic visions must be performed in meetings, MOUs, and press releases to generate commitment. Thus, governance is an ongoing performance of consensus-building.
•	Discourse & Legitimacy: The political domain also involves performative speech-acts and narratives. Declaring “Rotterdam will be the world’s sustainable port-city” in a policy document is a speech act that commits the city to a course (Austin, 1962). But to avoid being “unhappy” (in Austin’s terms), it requires follow-through. City leaders engage in rituals of credibility – ribbon-cuttings for green facilities, public reports on progress – to perform legitimacy and accountability. These repeated acts (annual “State of the Port” addresses focusing on climate action, etc.) gradually constitute a new political reality where sustainability is an expectation. In essence, the governance culture shifts: formerly, success was tonnage growth; now it’s also emissions reduction and community well-being, and this shift is solidified by the performance of new metrics and awards (e.g., Rotterdam won a “Global Green Port” award, which further enacts legitimacy).
•	Policy as Iterative Learning: Performativity highlights that policies are not one-off edicts but iterative practices. Rotterdam’s climate adaptation strategy is updated continuously, treating each implemented project as a learning performance (e.g., testing a floating pavilion gave input for scaling up floating housing policy). This trial-and-error, or “learning-by-doing,” is itself a performative mechanism (Argyris & Schön, 1978) – the city performs small experiments that shape the eventual policies. It blurs the line between implementation and formulation of policy. The performative outcome is more resilient governance that evolves with the system it governs.
•	Key References: Callon (1986) on translation in policy networks; Latour (2005) on politics of things; Bulkeley & Castán Broto (2013) on urban experimentation; Hajer (2005) on performative policy framing; Healey (1998) on collaborative planning as performance.
Cultural Performativity Domain
•	Cultural Scripts and Rituals: Cultural performativity in the port city refers to how meaning, identity, and community practices are actively produced. Culture is not a static heritage; it’s continuously re-written through performances (Schechner, 2003). In Rotterdam’s shift, new cultural scripts have emerged around sustainability and maritime identity. For example, community workshops at BlueCity Lab involve citizens in making bio-materials or upcycled products. These workshops function as performative pedagogy: people embody the abstract ideas of circularity by literally doing them (hands-on crafting, experimenting). Over time, such repeated acts instill new cultural norms (e.g., “Rotterdammers value reuse and water innovation”). The city’s cultural identity as a innovative “blue city” is thus a result of countless small performances by individuals and groups aligning with that narrative.
•	Embodiment and Intra-Corporeality: The term transcorporeality (Alaimo, 2010) is apt – recognizing the flow of substances and influences across bodies and environment. In port city culture, this appears in, say, local diets incorporating sustainable seafood from the port’s aquaculture or people practicing “urban swimming” in clean harbor basins. By literally incorporating the environment into bodies or placing bodies into the environment, the separation of human and nature dissolves in lived experience. These acts are symbolic (demonstrating humans can coexist with a cleaner water) and material (improving health, etc.). The yearly Rotterdam Water Games, where families kayak and swim in the old harbor, perform a reclaimed relationship with the water, erasing stigmas of the polluted, off-limits docks of the past. Such cultural performances make the blue economy tangible and emotionally resonant.
•	Narratives and Imaginaries: Stories, art, festivals, and media portrayals are cultural performances that shape collective imagination. Rotterdam’s museums and tours now highlight both the glory of its shipping past and the vision of a sustainable maritime future. The narrative of transformation – from a “grey” industrial port to a “blue” vibrant city – is reinforced by events like the World Port Days where new tech (e.g., hydrogen boats, algae cocktails) are showcased alongside traditional ship parades. This blending of old and new in a performative public spectacle helps society internalize the change. Each retelling (in press, social media, local school curriculums) of Rotterdam’s blue innovations is a speech act that reinforces a cultural consensus: “We are a forward-looking, water-positive city.” Over time, these narratives shape identity – residents start seeing themselves as agents of a maritime sustainable future, which in turn encourages behaviors consistent with that (voting for green policies, pursuing jobs in the blue economy, etc.).
•	Artistic Interventions: Artists and cultural practitioners often act as catalysts in performative shifts by staging provocative works. In Rotterdam, examples include the Waterfront theater performances and art installations like a floating art pavilion or a “plastic fishing” sculpture. These works create spaces of reflection and symbolize new relationships (e.g., a ballet performed on a sea platform dramatizes human-ocean harmony). Through art (which is inherently performative), complex ideas of wet ontology or circular economy are translated into affective experiences, accelerating cultural acceptance. As Schechner notes, performances can “bend time, reshape and adorn the environment” (Schechner, 1985) – here, cultural performances bend the trajectory of the city by envisioning alternative futures.
•	Key References: Schechner (2003) on performance in culture; Turner (1982) on social dramas; Alaimo (2010) on transcorporeality; Hawkins (2014) on art and environment; James (2015) on urban imaginaries.
Synthesis: The Blue Economy Blueprint in Rotterdam
•	Constitutive Integration: Across these domains, Rotterdam’s performative shifts have coalesced into a new integrated framework – a Blue Economy Blueprint. It is characterized by socio-ecological and political-economic integration, meaning environmental and social objectives are embedded into economic and governance structures, not treated separately. The port-city interface has transformed from a line of division into a zone of synergy (“port-city symbiosis”). Concrete outcomes include mixed-use waterfronts where businesses and nature restoration projects sit side by side, and circular economy incubators that bridge municipal waste services with startup companies. This integration is performed daily: city officials coordinate with port engineers on climate projects, community groups work with companies on reusing waste, etc., weaving a single fabric of action.
•	Performative Constellations: We can identify four performative constellations in Rotterdam’s blueprint, each corresponding to the domains but linked together:
1.	Economic Constellation: Circular value chains linking waste, resources, and culture (e.g., BlueCity ecosystem) – demonstrating that economic development can be regenerative.
2.	Technological Constellation: Floating and smart infrastructures enabling climate adaptation (e.g., floating dairy, autonomous electric barges) – showing technology can embed resilience and connect city with water.
3.	Political Constellation: Multi-scalar governance networks (EU programs, regional coalitions, city initiatives) coordinating the sustainability transition – illustrating new forms of collaborative governance that transcend silos.
4.	Cultural Constellation: Embodied learning and blue identity formation (education, public engagements, art) – creating an ethos of innovation and stewardship around water.
Each constellation is a cluster of projects, policies, and practices that together perform a particular aspect of the Blue City. For instance, the Floating Farm (tech) is linked to an educational visitor center (culture), supported by city policy incentives (politics), and connected to circular feed and energy loops (economic). It’s in these intersections that the real innovation happens.
•	Adaptive Cycle: Rotterdam’s blueprint also implies a methodology: an adaptive performative cycle. Rather than a fixed master plan, the city uses a learn-adjust loop. Trial projects (pilot algae farms, experimental wharf gardens) are evaluated and scaled if successful – performing as prototypes that gradually normalize new approaches. This keeps the transformation process flexible and responsive to feedback (e.g., community responses, technological breakthroughs, climate data). Such adaptiveness is key to resilience theory (Folke et al., 2010) and is visibly performed in Rotterdam’s incremental but steady trajectory from grey to blue.
•	Exportable Pattern: The framework emerging in Rotterdam serves as a “pattern example” (Flyvbjerg, 2011) for other port cities. It demonstrates that through performative interventions – tangible projects plus enabling narratives – a city can pivot from a linear, segregated model to a circular, integrated model. Already, knowledge exchange networks (like the C40 Ports initiative, or EU’s Sustainable Blue Economy Partnership funding 24 projects across 25 countries) are taking note of Rotterdam’s successes and challenges. The blueprint is not a one-size template, but a set of principles: engage multi-level actors, encourage experimental projects, integrate domains, and always pair physical changes with cultural change. These principles, if performed in context-sensitive ways elsewhere, can potentially replicate Rotterdam’s outcomes – cleaner environment, new jobs in green tech, improved city-port relations, and a populace that embraces the maritime identity of their city.
Conclusion: Toward a Wet Performativity Future
•	Water as Actor: In re-imagining Rotterdam, water itself has become an acknowledged actor in urban development, not merely a backdrop. This represents a profound ontological shift. The city now plans with the water – tides, storms, and flows are integral to designs. As Steinberg & Peters (2015) would put it, Rotterdam is learning to “think with the sea”. Wet performativity means every urban performance (be it economic production or cultural festivals) takes the water milieu into account. This paves the way for climate adaptation and ecological harmony to be sustained not as external constraints, but as internalized parts of urban life.
•	More-than-Human City: Non-human participants (from algae to cows to algorithms) are actively shaping Rotterdam’s trajectory. The success of projects like BlueCity or Floating Farm has shown that including biosystems in design can create robust, innovative solutions. It challenges anthropocentrism: the city is a co-creation of human and non-human forces. This ethos, rooted in Barad’s agential realism, fosters humility and creativity – citizens and officials alike learn from nature’s performance (e.g., mimicking ecosystems’ circularity).
•	Performative Governance and Collaboration: Governance in Rotterdam’s blueprint is about doing together. Public, private, academic, and community actors engage in joint performances – living labs, public-private pilots, consensus workshops – rather than top-down mandates. This builds trust and shared ownership of the outcomes. The performative approach to policy (try, demonstrate, iterate) also increases legitimacy: stakeholders see results in action, not just on paper, which galvanizes further support. Other port cities are taking note that process matters as much as product – how you get there (involving people, staging visible successes) can determine long-term adoption.
•	Cultural Transformation: Perhaps the most encouraging sign is the change in mindset among Rotterdam’s people. The emerging “blue culture” values sustainability, innovation, and connection to the water. This cultural shift means the changes are likely to be enduring – they’re not just projects imposed from above, but a new normal embraced from below. Each child that attends a maritime workshop or each resident who sees the new algae park contributes to a collective narrative that “we are champions of the blue economy.” Culture is the fabric that binds the economic, tech, and political threads into a coherent garment. In Rotterdam, that fabric now has the hue of the ocean.
•	Equitable & Resilient Futures: Ultimately, the Rotterdam case illustrates that a performative approach can lead to more equitable and resilient futures. By focusing on enactment, the city ensured that high-level goals (resilience, inclusivity) translated into on-the-ground benefits: job opportunities in new sectors, cleaner air and water in city neighborhoods, community empowerment through participation. It embodies the idea that sustainability is not achieved by fiat, but by performance – a continuous, inclusive process of making and remaking our cities. The Blue Economy Blueprint thus serves as both an inspiration and a provocation: a call for other cities to script their own performances of change, embracing the uncertainty and creativity of the process, and daring to move beyond the containerized logic of the past into the fluid, living urban seascape of the future.


Jak miasto okazuje to, że jest portowe? – Zgiełk. Performatywność przestrzeni (site-performance?) 
Jak portowość miasta aktywizuje dziś ludzi do działania? Portowość czyli obecne i dzisiejsze rezultaty działań pod wpływem bliskości morza i obecności portu
Jakie działania sprawiają, że miasto jest portowe? Działania racjonalizowane przez interesy (korzyść), a więc orientowane przez interesy (celowość bezpośrednią), ale często z udziałem odwołania do wartości, emocji i tradycji (sens, znaczenie). Korzystne i słuszne.
Działania społeczne świadczące o portowości miasta są więc jawne (performatywne) i celowe (racjonalne), zorientowane na interes (korzyść), wartość (słuszność), emocje (afektywność, spontaniczność) lub tradycję (uzasadnione). 
Działania performatywne (performans) mogą przebiegać na poziomie jednostkowym, wtedy powodowane są przez potrzeby jednostki i pozostają w sferze prywanej. Mogą przebiegać na poziomie 
	Poziom	Sfera	Motywator
	jednostkowy	Prywatna	Potrzeby
		Niepubliczna (nieujawniona publicznie)	Interesy
		Publiczna	Roszczenia
	grupowy	Prywatna	Potrzeby
		Niepubliczne (nieujawniona publicznie)	Interesy
		Publiczne	Roszczenia

Działanie (do ludzi) a performans (do ludzi i nie-ludzi)


•	Piotr Urbanowicz 2017 Działanie: Karen Barad w artykule „Ku zrozumieniu, jak materia zaczyna mieć znaczenie” (Barad, K. (2003). Posthumanist performativity: Toward an understanding of how matter comes to matter. Signs: Journal of women in culture and society, 28(3), 801-831.) Barad 2012: Karen Barad, Posthumanistyczna performatywność. Ku zrozumieniu, jak materia zaczyna mieć znaczenie (2003), przeł. Joanna Bednarek [w:] Teorie wywrotowe. Antologia przekładów, red. Agnieszka Gajewska, Wydawnictwo Poznańskie, Poznań 2012, s. 32; Problem performatywności materii (maszyn, przyrządów i obiektów) zaznacza się, w tym że Harry Collins i Steven Yearley argumentowali, że choć materia oddziałuje na sytuację konstruowania przedmiotu to sprawczość lokuje się po stronie ludzkiego podmiotu. Tymczasem autorzy koncepcji ANT (Actor-Network Theory) rozbili odpowiedzialność na konstruowanie znaczenia na wiele aktorów – do których zaliczają się nie-ludzie. Obrazuje to proces przejścia od podejścia humanistycznego (Pickering 1995:26) do posthumanistycznego sposobu widzenia sprawczości. Pojęcie sprawczości staje się tutaj najważniejsze. Jako pewna właściwość determinuje działanie. Podmiot, któremu nie przyznajemy sprawczości, nie może działać. W klasycznej socjologii sprawczość posiadał racjonalny i świadomy podmiot, po zwrocie posthumanistycznym natomiast jest ona raczej rozumiana jako suma mniejszych procesów. Barad proponuje paradygmat performatywny. Oznacza to przestawienie ontologicznych wyznaczników podmiotu i charakteru jego relacji z otoczeniem. Ontologicznie pierwotne mają być relacje, a nie indywidua. Byty, jakie znamy, nie istnieją poza relacją z innymi. Nie są, a jawią się nam. W tym sensie, pisze badaczka, rzeczywistość nie składa się z rzeczy-w-sobie, ani rzeczy-poza-fenomenami, ale z rzeczy-w-fenomenach (Barad 2012:343). Działanie nie jest więc czynnością, ale dokonuje się niejako samoistnie, poza indywiduum, bez jego pełnej kontroli. Działanie, jeśli już dostrzeżone, zostaje uchwycone tylko w jednym aspekcie, jawi się jako jednorodny proces (nad przypadk jednego czynnika), podczas gdy jest raczej sumą wielu korelacji. 
•	E. Domańska, „Zwrot performatywny” we współczesnej humanistyce, „Teksty Drugie” 2007, nr 5, s. 48-61: Zwrot performatywny niesie za sobą wejście w metaforę rozumienia świata jako wielości performatywnych działań i jako performance’u, w którym się uczestniczy (s.52). Performanse to zachowane zachowania (restored behaviours). Tym samym działanie o charakterze performansu stanowi powene powtórzenie – jego źródło najduje się poza daną jednostką, dzięki skontekstualizoaniu oraz ucieleśnieniu, staje się on unikatowy. 
•	Hołda, R. (2015). Miasto jako przestrzeń performansu kulturowego. Studia Etnologiczne i Antropologiczne, (15), 131-143. Zachowane zachowanie” to postępowanie, czynność, proces lub działanie utrwalone kulturowo i odtwarzane według pewnego — mniej lub bardziej ściśle określonego — scenariusza, zakładającego jednak możliwość pewnej zmiany R. scHecHner: Performatyka. Wstęp. Przeł. T. kubikowski. Wrocław 2006, s. 50—51.

Według Marvina Carlsona: „Sztuki performatywne wymagają fizycznej obec-ności wyszkolonych czy wprawnych istot ludzkich, które demonstrując swoje umiejętności, tworzą performans”4. Precyzuje: „W »performansie« — »występie« — nieodzowne jest zazwyczaj ludzkie działanie [...]; naprawdę jednak liczy się publiczna demonstracja konkretnych umiejętności”5 M. CarLson: Performans. Przeł. E. kubikowska. Warszawa 2007, s. 25 i 26. W sposób szczególny interesuje mnie performans ujmowany jako świadome działanie podejmowane w przestrzeni publicznej, niebędącej typową przestrze-nią teatralną lub wystawienniczą. Oznacza to nacisk na pewien typ działalności artystycznej prowadzonej poza obrębem budynków uznawanych niegdyś za świą-tynie sztuki (gmachy teatralne, galerie, muzea) i przeniesienie jej do przestrzeni życia codziennego, do miejsc, w których przebywają lub które odwiedzają ludzie w trakcie wykonywania rutynowych czynności. Zmiana taka w istotny sposób wpływa na sytuację prezentacji i odbioru widowiska. Przestrzeń, w której dzieje się przedstawienie, w tym także przestrzeń miejska, jeżeli jest wykorzystywana w tym celu, szczególnie wpływa na relacje między aktorami a widzami oraz na sposoby postrzegania tego, co się w niej dzieje6.
•	Piotr Urbanowicz 2017 Działanie: Mimo że Barad pisze o relacji, ostentacyjnie rezygnuje z pojęcia interakcji na rzecz intra-akcji. W przeciwieństwie do tego, co głosił na przykład Goff man, byty nie wchodzą ze sobą w relacje, przyczyniając się tym samym do produkcji znaczenia. Intra-akcja odbywa się wewnątrz fenomenu. Jej konsekwencją jest semiotyczne rozdzielenie składników fenomenu. Kierunek działania okazuje się więc odwrotny niż w interakcjonizmie, według którego pierwotnie istniejące byty wchodzą ze sobą w interakcje. W teorii Barad natomiast to pierwotna intra-akcja rozdziela przedmioty, czyni je widocznymi i oferuje człowiekowi możliwość nadania im oddzielnego znaczenia. To rozdzielenie nazywa w swojej teorii „cięciem sprawczym”. Jego funkcją jest dostarczenie „«lokalnego» rozwiązania «w obrębie» fenomenu ontologicznej nieoznaczoności” (Barad 2012: 340). Tylko to rozdzielenie ma istotną siłę sprawczą, a dalsze procesy społeczne i naukowe są wobec niego wtórne. Materia jawi się w tej teorii nie jako rzecz, ale „działanie, zastygła sprawczość” (Barad 2012: 349), którą człowiek może uchwycić w znaczeniu tylko za sprawą wykonanego przez materię sprawczego cięcia. Przy czym, jak zaznacza wyraźnie Barad, „sprawczość to nie atrybut, ale ciągłe rekonfi gurowanie się świata” (2012: 344; wyróżnienie moje – P.U.). Świat nieprzerwanie działa. W momencie dokonania cięcia to nie człowiek konfi guruje dany fenomen, lecz raczej świat daje mu się na chwilę uchwycić. Pojęcie chwili jest jednak względne, ponieważ nie ma żadnej ontologicznej wagi. Materia bowiem wciąż i na nowo (re)konfi guruje granice znaczeń przedmiotu lub zjawiska ustanowione przez pomiar. 
•	Filanowski, B. (2021). Badanie performansu kulturowego jako czynnika wpływającego na selekcję informacji przez media. In Metody badania komunikacji i mediów. Perspektywa teoretyczna i analityczna (pp. 317-326). Wydawnictwo Uniwersytetu Łódzkiego. Koncepcja J. McKenziego lokuje performans kulturowy w ramach ogólnej teorii performansu rozumianej jako metamodel sposobu funkcjonowania współczesnego świata. Obejmuje ona trzy pola ludzkiej i pozaludzkiej aktywności: działalność kulturową, organizacyjną i technologiczną. Performans kulturowy jest domeną performatyki, a  najważniejszym jego celem jest skuteczność (efficacy), performans organizacyjny jest domeną zarządzania performatywnego, a  jego celem jest wydajność (efficiency), natomiast technologiczny performans jest nakierowany na sprawność (effectiveness), osiąganą przez doskonalenie technoperformatywnych procedur. J.  McKenzie widzi ich wzajemne związki i  sieć relacji, jakie tworzą. W performansach dostrzega dwie tendencje: mutacyjność (nakierowanie na wprowadzanie zmian) i normatywność (utrzymywanie pożądanego stanu). Dostrzega między nimi sprzężenie zwrotne, polegające na tym, że zbyt mocne performowanie w stronę normalizacji sprzyja mutacyjności, a zbyt duże rozchwianie performansu sprzyja ponownej normalizacji.
•	Piotr Urbanowicz 2017 Działanie: Jak widać dzięki przywołanym tutaj przykładom, w swoich początkach performatyka i socjologia dzieliły pojęcie racjonalnego działania. Tworzy ono trzon słownika obu dyscyplin. O ile klasyczna socjologia rozumiejąca miała za zadanie „oglądać” poszczególne działania społeczne z bezstronnej pozycji, o tyle performatyka, opierając się na ustaleniach krytyki feministycznej, sproblematyzowała samą sytuację „okazywania” działania. Ponadto Schechner w koncepcji „zachowanego zachowania” rozwinął problem wpływu obcych czynników na działanie, obecny immanentnie i pretekstowo u Webera. Rdzeń racjonalnego działania i ludzkiej sprawczości pozostawał jednak nietknięty aż do czasu zwrotu performatywnego, dokonanego przede wszystkim w obrębie socjologii wiedzy. Wywarł on wpływ na badaczki feministyczne, zainteresowane szczególnie materialnością i procesualnością zjawisk kulturowych. Samo pojęcie działania odczepiło się niejako od podmiotu ludzkiego. W radykalnej teorii „posthumanistycznej performatywności” Karen Barad widać, jak autorka przesuwa akcenty w pojęciach związanych tradycyjnie z działaniem. Rozszerza także intuicję Webera o samo-dzielących się komórkach popychających człowieka do działania. Podważa totalność „interpretacji”, uwewnętrznia „interakcję” i oddaje sprawczość materii, nadając działaniu zupełnie nowe, odmienne od tradycyjnego znaczenie
•	Snarska, A. (2016). Budowanie marki jako performans. Literatura i Kultura Popularna, 22, 111-125. Wojciech Dudzik, cytując Richarda Schechnera, definiuje performans jako każdy występ jednostki wobec innych i wylicza: realizacja roli społecznej, pokaz umiejętności, działania rytualne o charakterze religijnym, rozrywka, widowiska, eventy, ceremonia, przemarsz, pielgrzymka, gra, zabawa, wojna, sposoby sprawowania władzy, egzekucja, uzdrawianie itd., itp. — wszystkie działania, dzięki którym stajemy się tymi, kim jesteśmy jako reprezentanci danej kultury i jako tacy możemy być postrzegani, opisywani, rozumiani przez innych5
•	Site-specific natomiast jest wejściem w gotową lokalizację, generalnie niebędącą miejscem teatralnym, w której ingerencje artystów w samo miejsce (jego fizyczne warunki, w tym w architekturę) są żadne bądź minimalne i to właśnie lokalizacja determinuje kształt dzieła. Inaczej mówiąc, przestrzeń przedstawienia site-specific to przestrzeń hybrydyczna, w której trudno jednoznacznie oddzielić od siebie działanie artystyczne i to, co zwykliśmy nazywać przestrzenią pozaartystyczną. Dyskurs performatyczny odchodzi więc od definiowania sztuki site-specific jako określonego gatunku sztuki, z wyraźnie zaznaczonym kierunkiem oddziaływania konkretnej przestrzeni na działanie artystyczne. W takim przedstawieniu mamy raczej do czynienia z wielością rozmaitych, wzajemnych oddziaływań między przedstawieniem i przestrzenią, w której się ono rozgrywa.
